Essential Health Finds
Sponsored
I am a sixty-two-year-old retired letter carrier in Vermont, and I am going to tell you about a coffee mug. 

My name is Edward.

People have called me Ed since I was nine years old.

I am sixty-two.

I live with my wife in a small house outside Burlington, Vermont.

I was a letter carrier for thirty-seven years.

I walked a rural route in northern Vermont for thirty-one of those years, and a smaller in-town route for the last six.

I retired at sixty after a hip surgery that healed cleanly.

I am not on any prescription medications.

My doctor — Dr. Foster, a careful woman I have been seeing for eleven years — tells me at every annual physical that my bloodwork is in the ranges she wants it in.

I sleep seven hours a night.

I walk my dog a mile and a quarter every morning.

I eat the way a man my age should eat, which is to say less than I want to and more vegetables than I would choose.

I am, on paper, a sixty-two-year-old man in fine shape.

That is the part I want you to hold while you read this.

I want to tell you, plainly, what I started to notice in the second winter after I retired.

It was small.

It would not have registered on any chart.

I am not telling you a dramatic story.

I am telling you what one careful man started to notice in his own kitchen on a cold morning when his wife was upstairs and the coffee was ready and he was the only one to notice it.

It started with my coffee mug.

I have a heavy stoneware mug that has been my morning mug for almost twenty-five years.

I bought it at a craft fair when our daughter was eleven.

It is the only mug I drink coffee out of in the morning.

I had been wrapping both hands around it for almost a year before I realized I was doing it.

I had not been doing that for the first twenty-three years I had the mug.

I had been holding it with one hand, the way most people hold a coffee mug, and reading the paper with the other.

Somewhere in there I had become a two-hand-around-the-mug man.

I noticed one morning that I was doing it.

I sat there at my own kitchen table and I asked myself, honestly, when I had started.

I could not tell you.

That was the first thing.

The second thing was the driveway.

I bring the mail in the afternoons, mostly out of habit.

A man who carried mail for thirty-seven years does not let his own mail sit in a box at the end of his driveway for very long.

Our driveway is about a hundred and forty yards.

It is unpaved.

It has a small hill in the middle of it.

I had been doing that walk in three or four minutes for as long as I had owned the house, which is twenty-six years.

Somewhere in the year before my second retirement winter, I had started to pause at the top of the hill.

I would stand there for thirty seconds with my hands in my pockets.

I would tell myself I was just looking at the trees.

I had not stood at the top of the driveway and looked at the trees in twenty-five years.

I had started to do it just about every afternoon.

I started to notice that, too.

The third thing was at the workbench.

I have a small workbench in our garage.

It is where I keep my hand tools, my coping saw, my small chisels, and the wood I am working on.

I am not a carpenter. I do small things — picture frames, a small step stool for our granddaughter, a birdhouse for the kitchen window — that take an evening or two.

I had been doing that work at the workbench in the garage on Saturday mornings for as long as we had lived in the house.

In the winter, the garage is cold. I have a small electric heater I run when I am out there.

Two winters ago, I started having trouble working the tools.

My hands were cold in the garage even with the heater going.

I started having to come back into the kitchen to warm them up every twenty minutes.

I stopped going out there.

The workbench sat empty for fourteen months.

I told my wife it was that I had run out of small projects.

I had not run out of small projects.

I had a piece of pine in there I had wanted to make into a small box for almost a year.

I had not gone out and made it.

I sat with all of this for a winter.

I went in for my annual physical in March.

Dr. Foster did the bloodwork.

I sat across from her at her desk a week later and we went over the results.

Everything was in the ranges she wanted it in.

Not borderline. Not perfect. In the ranges.

She looked at me at the end.

She said: anything we should be talking about, Ed?

I told her about the coffee mug.

I told her about the driveway.

I told her about the workbench.

I told her my hands felt different than they had at fifty-five, and that I was not asking for an answer, but I wanted her to have the picture.

She listened.

She wrote it down.

She said: I do not see anything on your chart I would do anything about today. But I appreciate you telling me. That is your body and you know it better than I do. If something changes, or if you want to come back sooner, you call me.

I told her thank you.

I left.

I drove home thinking about that conversation.

What I drove home thinking was this.

The chart was not wrong.

I was not wrong either.

There is a kind of slow shift that does not show up on the chart yet.

A man in his early sixties can be in fine shape by every measure his doctor can take and still be noticing that his hands are different in his own kitchen.

It is not a thing to panic about.

It is not a thing to ignore.

It is, I decided that afternoon, a thing to pay attention to.

I started reading.

I read for almost three weeks.

I am, by temperament, a careful man.

A man who walks rural mail routes for thirty-one years is, almost by definition, a careful man.

I do not put anything into my body without understanding what I am putting in.

I read about beetroot.

I read about the deep crimson pigment in the root.

It is called betalain.

It is the color that stains your cutting board for three days.

It is also, according to a long and patient body of research the cardiovascular community has been quietly building for decades, one of the more potent antioxidants in the food supply.

I read about the long, slowly built relationship the structure-function research has had with this vegetable.

In the context of supporting healthy circulation.

Supporting cardiovascular wellness.

Supporting the body's own cellular energy.

In people whose numbers are already in the range their doctors want them in.

None of what I read was a fireworks story.

It was the opposite of one.

It was a vegetable that has been quietly studied for a long time by careful people who were not making outlandish claims.

The language being used in the careful, structure-function corner of the conversation was the language I had been waiting to find.

Supports healthy circulation.

Not improves circulation.

Not fixes cold hands.

Not treats anything.

Just supports.

In people whose numbers are already in the ranges their doctors want them in.

That was the shape of the language I had been hoping to find.

I came across a small company called Rosabella.

I want to tell you what I liked about it, plainly.

The first thing was the chemistry.

The betalain pigment is heat-sensitive.

If you process beetroot the standard way, with heat, you lose some of the compound to the heat.

Rosabella cold-presses at low temperatures because they want as much of the compound as possible to make it from the field into the capsule.

That is a slower way to make a supplement.

It costs them more.

They made the choice anyway.

At sixty-two, after thirty-seven years of watching small businesses on my route open and close and the slow careful ones outlast the loud ones, I have come to pay close attention to the small decisions companies make when no one is watching.

Those decisions tell you everything.

The second thing I liked was the simplicity.

Two capsules in the morning with a glass of water.

That is the whole protocol.

I am a sixty-two-year-old retired letter carrier.

I am not interested in a protocol that requires its own shelf.

Now I am going to tell you the most important part of this letter, the part I want every man my age who reads this to hear.

Before I added anything to what I was taking, I called my pharmacist.

His name is Stephen.

He has been the pharmacist at the small independent pharmacy in our town for twelve years.

I have been filling things there for about as long.

I called him for a specific reason.

I am on no prescription medications.

I take a multivitamin and a vitamin D and that is the entirety of my pill organizer.

I called Stephen anyway.

I called because I am a careful man and because I wanted somebody who knew what was on my record to confirm that adding a beetroot capsule to a multivitamin was the right thing for a man my age to do.

He pulled up my profile.

He confirmed there were no concerns at the dosage on the label.

He told me to take it in the morning with my coffee the way the bottle suggested.

He told me to call him back if Dr. Foster ever started me on any prescription medication in the future, because the conversation would be different at that point.

He told me, before he got off the phone, that he wished more of his patients made the call I had just made.

I also called Dr. Foster's office that week.

I left a message with her nurse, with the same information.

She called me back the next day to tell me Dr. Foster had no concerns about my adding it at the dosage on the label.

I want to say something now, plainly, to every reader of this letter.

If you are on any prescription medication, especially anything cardiovascular, please make the call before you add anything new.

I called Stephen.

I called Dr. Foster's office.

I would have made a third call if I had been on a prescription.

So should you.

The two minutes the call takes is the most important two minutes of your engagement with any supplement.

I started on a Monday in April.

I set the bottle on the counter next to the coffee maker.

I took the two capsules with my first sip of coffee.

The first two weeks I felt nothing in particular.

I had read enough to know that bodies are different.

Some men notice something within the first week or two.

Some men take much longer.

Some men don't notice much at all.

I was prepared to land in any of those camps.

I was prepared to be honest with myself about the result.

What I noticed, I noticed slowly.

The first thing I noticed, around week six, was the coffee mug.

It was a Wednesday morning.

I was at the kitchen table.

The paper was open in front of me.

I had my coffee in my right hand.

My left hand was on the paper.

I sat with that for a moment.

I had not been a one-hand-on-the-mug man for almost a year.

I had been quietly two-handing my coffee for that long.

I had not, that morning, gone to put my second hand on the mug.

I had been reading the front page for ten minutes with the paper in my left hand and the mug in my right.

I did not say anything about it.

I did not want to make a thing of it.

I had read enough not to make a thing of one observation.

I made a quiet note.

I kept paying attention.

The second thing I noticed, around week nine, was the driveway.

It was a Sunday afternoon in early May.

I went out to bring in the mail.

I walked down the driveway with my hands at my sides.

I went to the box, took the mail out, turned around, and walked back up.

I came in the kitchen door.

My wife was at the table.

She asked me what I was smiling about.

I had not realized I was smiling.

I had not paused at the top of the hill.

I had walked up the driveway at the speed I had walked up the driveway for twenty-five years.

I had not done that in over a year.

I told her about the coffee mug, too.

She told me she had been waiting for me to notice the coffee mug.

She had been watching me, the way wives watch.

The third thing I noticed was the workbench.

It was a Saturday morning in late May.

It was cool outside but not cold.

The garage door was closed.

I had the small electric heater going.

I had the piece of pine I had been wanting to make into a small box for our daughter sitting on the workbench.

I worked on it for an hour and a half.

Without gloves.

My hands worked the coping saw and the chisels the way they had worked them for thirty years.

When I came in for lunch, my wife looked at the sawdust on my sleeves and she did not say anything.

She does not say anything about most things she notices.

She just sees them.

I went back out after lunch and I worked for another hour.

I finished the box the following Saturday.

It is on our daughter's dresser now.

I want to be careful with what I am telling you.

I am not telling you Rosabella did any of those things on its own.

I had been walking more deliberately for several weeks before the driveway.

I had started, on my own, the day I came home from Dr. Foster's office in March, to pay attention to my hands and to my breath and to the small things I had been letting go of.

I had been doing the careful things I had been doing for forty years, just a little more carefully.

Something had unlocked in me the afternoon I left Dr. Foster's office that had nothing to do with a capsule on a saucer.

There were several things changing at once, the way there are when a careful man finally decides to start reading his own body again, after a year of pretending not to.

All I am telling you is that I added one small thing to a long list of things I was newly doing.

I kept everything else exactly the same.

And a stretch of months followed that was qualitatively different from the year that had preceded it.

That is the honest version.

I would not give you a cleaner one.

I went into Dr. Foster's office for a follow-up about six months after I started.

She did not have to repeat the bloodwork.

She looked at me.

She asked me how I was.

I told her about the coffee mug and the driveway and the workbench.

I told her about the beetroot.

I told her I had called Stephen before I started.

She nodded.

She said: that was the right way to go about it. The research on antioxidant support is good. Keep doing what you are doing and let's check in at your usual time.

I drove home from her office that afternoon.

I had been driving home from Dr. Foster's office for eleven years.

This was the first time I had driven home thinking that my hands on the steering wheel felt the way they used to feel.

I am telling you all of this because I think you might be where I was the morning I noticed the coffee mug.

Sitting at your own kitchen table.

In your own warm kitchen.

In your own fine shape, on paper.

Noticing that something quiet has shifted in the way you live in your own body.

If you are a man in your sixties — or a woman reading this for a man you love, the way my wife would read it for me — who has been telling himself it is just the winter, or just the sixties, or just retirement, I want to say a few things plainly.

I do not know your bloodwork or your medications or what your doctor has told you.

Some men don't notice much at all.

It is not a treatment for cold feet.

Living With Macular Degeneration
Sponsored
I've Run OCT Scans on AMD Patients for 22 Years. Here's Why I Stopped Trusting AREDS Alone.

I work alongside retina specialists every day. I'm the one who positions patients behind the machine, captures the scan, and pulls up the image before the doctor walks in.

I've seen more maculas than most ophthalmologists.

And I've watched AREDS fail.

Not fail like a switch flipping off. Fail slowly. Quietly. The kind of failure you only notice when you line up ten years of scans side by side and realize the patient who took every single pill, never missed a day, showed up for every appointment — is still losing their central vision.

I've watched it happen to hundreds of patients.

Mrs. Calloway. Eight years on AREDS. Her scans got a little worse every six months. Not dramatically. Just enough. She went from reading her own mail to needing her son to read it aloud. Eight years of compliance. Eight years of "stable." Eight years of slow, steady decline that everyone called "normal progression."

Mr. Oliveira. Eleven years. Started with 20/30. Ended at 20/100 in his better eye. Took his AREDS every morning like clockwork. His wife told me once at the front desk — he'd set an alarm so he never forgot.

Never forgot. Still progressed.

It's not that AREDS does nothing. Lutein and zeaxanthin are real. Zinc matters. The science behind the original formula was solid — for 1996.

But it's not enough.

I knew this professionally for years before it became personal.

Last spring, my colleague ran a routine scan on me. Just a quick check — one of the perks of working in a retina clinic. She positioned my chin on the rest. The green light swept across my eye.

She didn't say anything for a moment.

Then she pulled up the image.

Early dry AMD. Right eye. Small drusen deposits along the macula.

I've seen that scan a thousand times. This time it was mine.

I sat in the break room for fifteen minutes staring at a cup of coffee I didn't drink. Because I knew exactly what those deposits meant. I'd watched the progression play out on the other side of the OCT machine for twenty-two years.

And I'd watched it play out in my mother.

My mother was a PreserVision patient for twelve years. Faithful. She kept the blue box on the kitchen counter next to the coffee maker, and every morning she'd take her two soft gels with her orange juice. She never questioned it. Her retina specialist told her AREDS was the gold standard, and my mother trusted him completely.

The first thing she lost was the reading.

Not all at once. First the small print — medicine bottles, the church bulletin. She got a magnifier. Then the regular print. She got a bigger magnifier. Then even the large-print Reader's Digest started giving her trouble.

Then the faces.

Not strangers. Family. She'd squint at the dinner table like she was trying to see through fog. My father would sit three feet away and she'd ask him to move closer.

Then the Amsler grid lines started bending. Not a little. Dramatically — like someone had pressed their thumb into the center of the paper.

Then the DMV letter.

Then my nieces and nephews had to announce themselves when they walked into her room. "It's Katie, Grandma." "It's Jack." Because she couldn't tell them apart anymore. She could see a shape in the doorway. She could hear their voice. But the face — the one thing that tells you who a person IS — was gone.

Then the kitchen. The stove she'd cooked at for forty years became dangerous. She couldn't see the burner flame — whether it was on, whether it was high, whether her sleeve was close. My father disconnected it after she singed her wrist and didn't realize it until she smelled it.

She did everything they told her. Every pill, every appointment, every scan.

And she still can't see my face when I walk into her room.

That sentence has been sitting in my chest for three years. I've never said it out loud until now.

So when I saw those drusen on my own scan — my scan, my macula, my right eye — I didn't panic. I didn't cry.

I went home and made a decision.

I was going to take AREDS. I wasn't going to refuse the foundation. But I was not going to follow my mother's path. I was going to find what was missing.

I started reading the studies I'd never had time to read during clinic hours. Not the abstracts — the full papers. Fifteen years of AMD research that had accumulated while the AREDS protocol stayed frozen.

What I found made me furious.

Three root causes of macular degeneration. Not one. Three.

Oxidative stress — free radicals attacking the retinal pigment epithelium. This is what AREDS addresses. Lutein, zeaxanthin, zinc, vitamins C and E. The front door.

Compromised macular circulation — the tiny capillaries that feed the macula becoming stiff, narrowed, insufficient. Blood can't deliver nutrients, can't remove waste. AREDS has nothing for this.

Impaired cellular repair — the retinal cells losing their ability to regenerate, to clear damaged proteins, to maintain the photoreceptors that make central vision possible. AREDS has nothing for this either.

One pathway covered. Two completely ignored.

That's like locking your front door and leaving the back door and every window wide open.

No wonder 68% of AREDS patients still progress. They're protecting against one threat while two others walk in unchallenged.

I sat at my kitchen table with those studies spread across the surface and I wanted to call every patient I'd scanned that week and say: your protocol is incomplete. It's been incomplete for over a decade. The research exists. It just hasn't made it into the standard of care.

Margaret changed everything.

She'd been a patient at our clinic for nine years. Dry AMD, both eyes. I'd run her OCTs every six months like clockwork. I knew her scans better than my own handwriting.

She came in for her regular appointment, and I positioned her behind the machine. Chin rest, forehead strap, green light. The scan captured.

I pulled it up on the monitor.

No progression.

I blinked. Ran a comparison with her last three visits. Not just stable — the RPE layer looked slightly better. Slightly thicker. Slightly more uniform.

In nine years of scanning Margaret, I'd never seen her scans improve.

"Margaret," I said. "Did you change anything?"

She looked at me like she'd been waiting for someone to ask.

"I found something," she said. "About five months ago. Called Vision Enhance Pro."

I'd never heard of it.

"Most of those extra supplements are junk," Margaret said. "I tried two of them before this one. But this one — my retina specialist actually looked at the label."

She paused.

"He said, 'This is essentially AREDS plus about 20 years of additional research.'"

I asked Margaret for the name again. Wrote it on a sticky note. Stuck it in my scrub pocket.

That night, I sat at my kitchen table and evaluated Vision Enhance Pro the way I evaluate every clinical claim that comes through our office.

Twenty-four ingredients. Not six. Twenty-four.

Organized across all three pathways:

For oxidative protection — the AREDS foundation, yes. Lutein, zeaxanthin, zinc, vitamins C and E. But also lycopene, which crosses the blood-retinal barrier. Selenium. A complete B-vitamin complex.

For macular circulation — bilberry extract, which strengthens the retinal capillaries. Grape seed extract for blood vessel wall integrity. Rutin for healthy circulation to the smallest vessels. Quercetin for nutrient delivery to the macula itself.

For cellular repair — L-taurine, which concentrates in the retina more than any other tissue in the body. Alpha lipoic acid for nerve function and antioxidant regeneration. Eyebright for local tissue support.

Three pathways. All addressed. Not one.

Then I did something I should have done years ago. I went to the pharmacy and pulled the top five "advanced eye supplements" off the shelf. Read every label.

Three of them had no bilberry at all.

Two listed lutein — but at doses below what any study has ever tested.

One had eight ingredients total and charged more than AREDS.

That's when I understood why the two supplements I'd tried after my own diagnosis — one with eight ingredients, one with eleven — hadn't changed a thing on my three-month scan. They were incomplete. Wrong forms. Wrong doses. Close enough to look credible on the label, nowhere close to what the research actually supports.

I ordered Vision Enhance Pro that night.

The first two weeks — nothing. I expected that. Macular changes don't happen fast. You don't undo years of unchecked inflammation and vascular decline in fourteen days. I kept taking it alongside my AREDS. One protocol completing the other.

Week three.

Sunday morning at church. I picked up the hymnal. Started reading the lyrics without pulling the magnifier from my purse.

I stopped mid-verse. Looked at the page. Looked at my purse, still zipped shut.

Read the next line. Clear.

Week four. Same hymnal. Same clarity. Not a fluke.

Week six. I stayed late at the clinic and ran myself through the Amsler grid we keep in Exam Room 3. Covered my left eye. Stared at the center dot with my right.

The lines were less distorted.

I ran it again. Same result. The warping that had been spreading from the center — a little less. A little straighter. Not perfect. But unmistakably better than six weeks ago.

Month three.

I was standing at my kitchen window. Coffee in hand. Morning routine. Emma was crossing the backyard — my granddaughter, seven years old, on her way from the car to the back door.

I saw the ponytail.

I saw the red jacket.

Emma looked up and waved.

I waved back.

Then I stopped. Set my coffee down. Because six months ago, standing at this same window, I could see a shape in the yard. A small figure moving. But the ponytail. The jacket. The wave.

Those were gone six months ago. And now they were back.

Month four. Scheduled OCT at the clinic. My colleague ran the scan — the same colleague who'd found the drusen in the first place. She pulled up the comparison.

No progression. The drusen hadn't expanded. If anything, the RPE layer looked more uniform than it had at my last scan.

She called the retina specialist over. He looked at the images. Looked at my chart. Looked at the images again.

"Whatever you've added," he said. "Keep taking it. This is stable."

Stable.

After watching my mother's scans decline for twelve years on AREDS alone — stable felt like a miracle. Even though I know it's not a miracle. It's just what happens when you address all three pathways instead of one.

I called Tom that evening. My husband. I'd told him about the diagnosis, about the supplements, about the research. He'd been quiet about it. Patient. He's always patient.

There was a long silence on the phone when I told him the results.

Then he exhaled. The kind of exhale that sounds like someone putting down something very heavy.

"I was scared, Barb. I was really scared."

I know. I was too.

Last Saturday morning. Emma was in the yard again. Tom was making breakfast. I stood at the kitchen window with my coffee and watched my granddaughter chase the neighbor's cat across the grass.

I could see her face.

Not a shape. Not a blur. Emma. Laughing, hair flying, red jacket zipped up to her chin because it was cold and she never remembers to bring gloves.

I watched her and I thought: I am not following my mother's path.

If you've been taking AREDS for years and your scans keep getting worse — now you know why.

If your retina specialist says "stable" but you can tell things aren't stable — you're not imagining it.

If you've watched a parent disappear behind their own eyes and you can see yourself headed the same direction — you don't have to accept that.

If you've tried other "advanced" eye supplements and nothing changed — now you know why. Most of them don't address all three pathways. Most of them don't even come close.

Your protocol isn't failing you. It's just incomplete.

Vision Enhance Pro. Twenty-four ingredients. Three pathways. Everything AREDS should have been if the formula had kept up with the science.

Rosabella
Sponsored
There's a Flight of Stairs at My Granddaughter's School I'd Been Quietly Losing to for Two Years.

There's a flight of stairs at my granddaughter's elementary school.

Two flights, actually — twenty-two steps each, with a small landing between them.

The auditorium where her spring piano recital happens every May is at the top.

For her first three recitals I climbed those stairs without thinking about them.

At her fourth recital, two springs ago, I stopped on the landing.

Just for a moment.

I told myself I was admiring a watercolor that some fifth grader had taped to the wall.

At her fifth recital, last spring, I stopped on the landing again.

And then halfway up the second flight.

By the time I got to the auditorium I sat in my seat for two minutes before I could really focus on the program.

A man at least ten years older than me passed me on the way up.

He didn't stop.

I sat there in the auditorium that afternoon listening to children play "Für Elise" with one hand, and I made myself a small, careful promise.

The next time I saw my cardiologist, I was going to tell her about the stairs.

She looked across her desk at me a week later and said something I think about almost every day now.

She said: "Marilyn. Your bloodwork is in the ranges I want it in. Your medication is doing what we hoped it would do. But you're three years past the change now, and the conversation I want to have with you today is a little different from the ones we've been having."

Then she sat back in her chair and asked me — and these are her exact words — "what your body has been telling you that you haven't been telling me."

I sat there for a long moment.

I am sixty-four years old.

I was a high school guidance counselor for twenty-eight years before I retired.

I have spent most of my professional life asking other people careful questions and waiting for them to answer honestly.

I had not expected to be on the receiving end of one.

But I had walked into her office that morning prepared to answer one.

I told her about the stairs.

That was one thing.

The other thing was the hill to our church.

We've gone to the same small church for thirty-one years.

The parking lot is on the lower lawn. The church itself is at the top of a small slope — maybe forty paces, an incline you wouldn't call a hill if you were younger than fifty.

I had walked that slope every Sunday morning for thirty-one years without registering it.

Sometime in the year before my granddaughter's fifth recital, I had started parking in the upper lot instead, by the office.

It's only twenty paces, on the flat.

I told myself it was easier when I was carrying a casserole for the Sunday lunch.

I had not been carrying a casserole every Sunday for thirty-one years.

I had been carrying my body up a slope that was telling me something I was not in a mood to hear.

There was one more thing I told her too.

The way I sometimes notice my own heartbeat at night now, the way I didn't notice it ten years ago.

Not loudly. Not alarmingly.

Just present in a way it hadn't been present before.

I told her I knew none of it was anything, exactly.

My numbers were in the ranges. The medication was doing its work. The annual EKG had been unremarkable.

I told her I was probably making something out of nothing.

She did not say I was making something out of nothing.

She said: "Your numbers are in good shape, Marilyn. The medication is doing what we wanted it to do. A lot of women in their sixties start paying closer attention to what their bodies are telling them, especially the small physical things they used to do without noticing. That's a healthy instinct. The chart is one piece of information. What you've just told me is another. Both are useful."

She was quiet for a moment after that.

Then she said: "I'm not going to change anything today. Your medication is doing what we want it to do. But if you'd like to think about whether there's one small additive thing you might bring into your mornings — something general, structure-function, well-researched, that supports what your body is already doing — I'd be happy to have that conversation at your next appointment. Or sooner, if you call."

I drove home from her office that afternoon thinking about the difference between a doctor who treats a chart and a doctor who reads a person.

I started reading the next day.

I want to be clear about something before I describe what I read.

I am not a doctor.

I am not a nurse.

I do not have any medical expertise of any kind.

I am a retired high school guidance counselor with a husband, two grown sons, a granddaughter who plays piano, and a relationship with my own body that I have been paying closer attention to in recent years.

What follows is one careful woman's experience told the way I'd tell a friend at the kitchen table.

Nothing more.

I am a careful reader.

Twenty-eight years of working with teenagers and their parents teaches you to be suspicious of anything that arrives loud.

I skipped past the supplement companies promising things no supplement can promise.

I skipped past the protocols and the morning routines and the influencers and the people selling certainty.

I was looking for the careful, quiet, structure-function corner of the conversation.

The thing that kept coming up there was beetroot.

Not beetroot juice. Not the beetroot powders at the health food store.

The active compound in beetroot — a family of pigments called betalains.

The research is real.

It has been built carefully over decades by careful people who have not, to their credit, been making outlandish claims.

The betalains in beetroot are studied in the context of supporting healthy circulation, supporting cardiovascular wellness, and supporting the body's natural antioxidant balance — particularly in people whose numbers are already in the range their doctor wants them in.

That was the shape of the language I had been looking for.

Not "fixes." Not "treats." Not "reverses."

Just supports.

In women whose cardiovascular wellness was part of the long, careful conversation they were having with their own doctors.

One brand kept showing up in the more careful sources.

Rosabella.

The thing I liked about it was a small thing.

Betalains are heat-sensitive — that's just the chemistry of the molecule — and most processing methods involve high temperatures, which is the cheaper and faster way.

Rosabella cold-presses their beetroot at low temperatures.

It costs them more to do it that way.

It takes longer.

They do it anyway, because more of the active compound survives the processing.

After twenty-eight years of telling teenagers to pay attention to what people do rather than what they say, I have come to believe the same thing about companies.

The other thing I liked was the simplicity.

Two capsules in the morning with a glass of water.

That is the whole protocol.

I am sixty-four years old. I have already lived through the years of complicated routines.

I am not interested in another one.

Before I added it, I made two phone calls.

I want to talk about both, because they are the most important part of any honest letter about a supplement.

I am on a low-dose cholesterol medication. I have been on it for eight years.

My doctor and I are happy with how it has been working.

I would not add anything to that medication without checking first whether the addition was sensible — and "sensible" is not a thing I, as a retired guidance counselor in suburban Pittsburgh, am qualified to determine on my own.

I called Dr. Tanaka's office on a Tuesday morning.

I left a message with her nurse with the brand name and the dosage.

She called me back that afternoon.

Dr. Tanaka had said it was a reasonable thing to add, that there were no concerns with the dosage on the label alongside my current medication, and to let her know how I was feeling at our next appointment.

I called the pharmacy where I have been filling my prescription for nine years.

I told the pharmacist on duty what I wanted to add.

She pulled up my profile.

She confirmed what Dr. Tanaka had said — no concerns at the dosage on the label, given the medication and dose I am on.

She told me to take the capsules at the same time I take my morning prescription, with water, the way the bottle suggested.

I want to be plain about this.

Both calls together took me less than ten minutes.

If you are on any cardiovascular medication — a cholesterol medication, a blood pressure medication, a blood thinner, anything — those minutes are the most important minutes you will spend on the supplement.

The people who know what's on your chart will tell you in two sentences whether adding something is safe for you.

I am a retired guidance counselor in suburban Pittsburgh.

I am not the person to ask.

They are.

I started on a Monday in late April.

I put the bottle on the counter next to the coffee maker.

I took the two capsules with my first sip of coffee, right alongside my prescription.

I did not feel anything in particular for the first two weeks.

I had read enough to know I shouldn't expect to.

A supplement is not a drug. It doesn't switch on the way a drug switches on.

It is something the body uses, slowly, in the work the body is already doing.

What I noticed, I noticed in two specific moments.

The first one was at my granddaughter's end-of-year recital in June.

Same school. Same auditorium on the second floor. Same two flights of stairs.

I climbed them without stopping on the landing.

It wasn't that I climbed them quickly.

I didn't.

I climbed them the way I had climbed them at sixty-one, before I started quietly stopping on landings.

I got to the auditorium and I sat down in my seat and I noticed, with my purse in my lap, that I was not catching my breath.

I did not say anything to my husband.

He was already engrossed in the program.

I made a quiet note. I kept paying attention.

The second moment was on a Sunday morning in early August.

I was at the church parking lot.

I had a casserole in my hands — a real one this time, a chicken and wild rice for the women's group lunch.

I started toward the upper lot by the office, where I had been parking for over a year.

Then I stopped.

I turned around.

I walked back to the car.

I drove down to the lower lot and parked there instead.

I carried the casserole up the slope.

I did not stop.

I got to the top and I stood on the church porch for a moment and I noticed, with the casserole still in my hands, that I had walked up the slope I had been quietly refusing for a year and a half.

I did not tell anybody at church.

I went inside and put the casserole on the women's group table and sat down for the service.

That was the morning I let myself notice it.

I want to be careful with what I am saying here.

I am not telling you that Rosabella climbed those stairs or carried that casserole.

I had been doing other things alongside the supplement.

I had been walking a little more deliberately after my conversation with Dr. Tanaka.

I had been taking the steps in our own house at home, on purpose, three times a day, rather than avoiding them.

Something had shifted in me the afternoon I drove home from her office in April that had nothing to do with a capsule on a saucer.

There were several things changing at once.

The way there are when a careful woman finally tells a careful doctor the thing she has been carrying alone for two years.

What I can say is that I added one small thing to a list of small things I was newly doing.

I kept everything else exactly the same — including the medication Dr. Tanaka has me on.

And the months that followed were qualitatively different from the eighteen months that preceded them.

I saw Dr. Tanaka in October.

New bloodwork. New EKG.

She read the results for a few minutes.

Then she looked up.

She said: "Your numbers are in the same good place they've been. But you look like someone who hasn't been measuring her own breath lately."

I told her about the stairs and the casserole.

I told her about the beetroot.

I told her I'd called the office before I started.

She nodded.

She said: "That was the right way to do it. Antioxidant support in this range is well-researched. Keep going."

She did not change my medication.

She did not change anything else.

She told me she'd see me in March.

I drove home from her office that afternoon thinking that there is a quiet, careful conversation happening between a woman's body and her doctor in the years after the change.

And that the bloodwork only tells half of it.

The other half is what the body tells you on the stairs.

I'm telling you this because I think you might be a woman where I was two springs ago.

Post-menopausal.

In fine shape on paper.

On a medication that's doing its job.

With a quiet, persistent awareness that something has shifted in the way your body handles the small physical work of your day — the stairs, the slopes, the things you used to do without noticing them.

If that's you, please don't ignore what your body is telling you just because your chart is fine.

The chart is one piece of information.

Your body is another.

A good doctor wants both.

Mine asked me directly, and I'm grateful she did.

If yours hasn't, you can be the one to bring it up.

If you decide to think about adding a structure-function supplement to what you're already doing, please make the call before you start.

If you're on any cardiovascular medication — cholesterol, blood pressure, blood thinner, anything — this is not optional.

Call your doctor's office.

Call your pharmacist.

The two minutes it takes is the most important two minutes of your engagement with anything you put in your body.

The brand I added to my morning is Rosabella.

Two capsules with water, alongside my prescription, with my coffee.

1,300 milligrams of organic beetroot per serving, cold-pressed at low temperatures.

It is not a fix for anything.

It is not a substitute for hormone therapy or any cardiovascular medication.

It is not a treatment for menopause or for post-menopausal anything.

It is one small thing some women add to the long list of things they are already doing for themselves, alongside everything else.

Bodies are different.

Some women notice something within the first two weeks.

Some women take a couple of months.

Some women don't notice much at all.

That is the truthful version, and I would rather you hear it from me now than be disappointed later.

P.S. Rosabella has a ninety-day satisfaction guarantee.

Any reason at all.

You don't have to prove anything to anyone — not to them, not about your numbers, not about how you feel.

If you tried it and you're not satisfied, you're not satisfied.

They refund you.

That is the whole policy.

I appreciate that kind of policy at sixty-four.

P.P.S. They're a small operation working with a farming community in Lancaster County.

The cold-pressing takes longer than the conventional methods, so they sometimes run out between harvests.

I keep an extra bottle in the cupboard now.

I learned to stay one bottle ahead.

P.P.P.S. If you take only one thing from this letter, please take this:

Call your doctor's office and your pharmacist before you add anything new to a cardiovascular medication.

Not because anything bad will happen — probably nothing will.

Because the people who know your chart are the right people to confirm the addition is sensible for you.

The call takes two minutes.

Your doctor's office wants the call.

Your pharmacist wants the call.

Mine both said as much.

https://track.tryrosabella.com/cdc16426-3e48-40a7-9fad-21098084f6cf

DISCLAIMER

Individual experiences vary. These statements have not been evaluated by the FDA. This product is not intended to diagnose, treat, cure, or prevent any disease.

There's a Flight of Stairs at My Granddaughter's School I'd Been Quietly Losing to for Two Years.

He didn't stop.

The next time I saw my cardiologist, I was going to tell her about the stairs.

She looked across her desk at me a week later and said something I think about almost every day now.

She said: "Marilyn. Your bloodwork is in the ranges I want it in. Your medication is doing what we hoped it would do. But you're three years past the change now, and the conversation I want to have with you today is a little different from the ones we've been having."

Then she sat back in her chair and asked me — and these are her exact words — "what your body has been telling you that you haven't been telling me."

I sat there for a long moment.

I am sixty-four years old.

I was a high school guidance counselor for twenty-eight years before I retired.

I have spent most of my professional life asking other people careful questions and waiting for them to answer honestly.

I had not expected to be on the receiving end of one.

But I had walked into her office that morning prepared to answer one.

I told her about the stairs.

That was one thing.

The other thing was the hill to our church.

We've gone to the same small church for thirty-one years.

The parking lot is on the lower lawn. The church itself is at the top of a small slope — maybe forty paces, an incline you wouldn't call a hill if you were younger than fifty.

I had walked that slope every Sunday morning for thirty-one years without registering it.

Sometime in the year before my granddaughter's fifth recital, I had started parking in the upper lot instead, by the office.

It's only twenty paces, on the flat.

I told myself it was easier when I was carrying a casserole for the Sunday lunch.

I had not been carrying a casserole every Sunday for thirty-one years.

I had been carrying my body up a slope that was telling me something I was not in a mood to hear.

There was one more thing I told her too.

The way I sometimes notice my own heartbeat at night now, the way I didn't notice it ten years ago.

Not loudly. Not alarmingly.

Just present in a way it hadn't been present before.

I told her I knew none of it was anything, exactly.

My numbers were in the ranges. The medication was doing its work. The annual EKG had been unremarkable.

I told her I was probably making something out of nothing.

She did not say I was making something out of nothing.

She said: "Your numbers are in good shape, Marilyn. The medication is doing what we wanted it to do. A lot of women in their sixties start paying closer attention to what their bodies are telling them, especially the small physical things they used to do without noticing. That's a healthy instinct. The chart is one piece of information. What you've just told me is another. Both are useful."

She was quiet for a moment after that.

Then she said: "I'm not going to change anything today. Your medication is doing what we want it to do. But if you'd like to think about whether there's one small additive thing you might bring into your mornings — something general, structure-function, well-researched, that supports what your body is already doing — I'd be happy to have that conversation at your next appointment. Or sooner, if you call."

I drove home from her office that afternoon thinking about the difference between a doctor who treats a chart and a doctor who reads a person.

I started reading the next day.

I want to be clear about something before I describe what I read.

I am not a doctor.

I am not a nurse.

I do not have any medical expertise of any kind.

I am a retired high school guidance counselor with a husband, two grown sons, a granddaughter who plays piano, and a relationship with my own body that I have been paying closer attention to in recent years.

What follows is one careful woman's experience told the way I'd tell a friend at the kitchen table.

Nothing more.

I am a careful reader.

Twenty-eight years of working with teenagers and their parents teaches you to be suspicious of anything that arrives loud.

I skipped past the supplement companies promising things no supplement can promise.

I skipped past the protocols and the morning routines and the influencers and the people selling certainty.

I was looking for the careful, quiet, structure-function corner of the conversation.

The thing that kept coming up there was beetroot.

Not beetroot juice. Not the beetroot powders at the health food store.

The active compound in beetroot — a family of pigments called betalains.

The research is real.

It has been built carefully over decades by careful people who have not, to their credit, been making outlandish claims.

The betalains in beetroot are studied in the context of supporting healthy circulation, supporting cardiovascular wellness, and supporting the body's natural antioxidant balance — particularly in people whose numbers are already in the range their doctor wants them in.

That was the shape of the language I had been looking for.

Not "fixes." Not "treats." Not "reverses."

Just supports.

In women whose cardiovascular wellness was part of the long, careful conversation they were having with their own doctors.

One brand kept showing up in the more careful sources.

Rosabella.

The thing I liked about it was a small thing.

Betalains are heat-sensitive — that's just the chemistry of the molecule — and most processing methods involve high temperatures, which is the cheaper and faster way.

Rosabella cold-presses their beetroot at low temperatures.

It costs them more to do it that way.

It takes longer.

They do it anyway, because more of the active compound survives the processing.

After twenty-eight years of telling teenagers to pay attention to what people do rather than what they say, I have come to believe the same thing about companies.

The other thing I liked was the simplicity.

Two capsules in the morning with a glass of water.

That is the whole protocol.

I am sixty-four years old. I have already lived through the years of complicated routines.

I am not interested in another one.

Before I added it, I made two phone calls.

I want to talk about both, because they are the most important part of any honest letter about a supplement.

I am on a low-dose cholesterol medication. I have been on it for eight years.

My doctor and I are happy with how it has been working.

I would not add anything to that medication without checking first whether the addition was sensible — and "sensible" is not a thing I, as a retired guidance counselor in suburban Pittsburgh, am qualified to determine on my own.

I called Dr. Tanaka's office on a Tuesday morning.

I left a message with her nurse with the brand name and the dosage.

She called me back that afternoon.

Dr. Tanaka had said it was a reasonable thing to add, that there were no concerns with the dosage on the label alongside my current medication, and to let her know how I was feeling at our next appointment.

I called the pharmacy where I have been filling my prescription for nine years.

I told the pharmacist on duty what I wanted to add.

She pulled up my profile.

She confirmed what Dr. Tanaka had said — no concerns at the dosage on the label, given the medication and dose I am on.

She told me to take the capsules at the same time I take my morning prescription, with water, the way the bottle suggested.

I want to be plain about this.

Both calls together took me less than ten minutes.

If you are on any cardiovascular medication — a cholesterol medication, a blood pressure medication, a blood thinner, anything — those minutes are the most important minutes you will spend on the supplement.

The people who know what's on your chart will tell you in two sentences whether adding something is safe for you.

I am a retired guidance counselor in suburban Pittsburgh.

I am not the person to ask.

They are.

I started on a Monday in late April.

I put the bottle on the counter next to the coffee maker.

I took the two capsules with my first sip of coffee, right alongside my prescription.

I did not feel anything in particular for the first two weeks.

I had read enough to know I shouldn't expect to.

A supplement is not a drug. It doesn't switch on the way a drug switches on.

It is something the body uses, slowly, in the work the body is already doing.

What I noticed, I noticed in two specific moments.

The first one was at my granddaughter's end-of-year recital in June.

Same school. Same auditorium on the second floor. Same two flights of stairs.

I climbed them without stopping on the landing.

It wasn't that I climbed them quickly.

I didn't.

I climbed them the way I had climbed them at sixty-one, before I started quietly stopping on landings.

I got to the auditorium and I sat down in my seat and I noticed, with my purse in my lap, that I was not catching my breath.

I did not say anything to my husband.

He was already engrossed in the program.

I made a quiet note. I kept paying attention.

The second moment was on a Sunday morning in early August.

I was at the church parking lot.

I had a casserole in my hands — a real one this time, a chicken and wild rice for the women's group lunch.

I started toward the upper lot by the office, where I had been parking for over a year.

Then I stopped.

I turned around.

I walked back to the car.

I drove down to the lower lot and parked there instead.

I carried the casserole up the slope.

I did not stop.

I got to the top and I stood on the church porch for a moment and I noticed, with the casserole still in my hands, that I had walked up the slope I had been quietly refusing for a year and a half.

I did not tell anybody at church.

I went inside and put the casserole on the women's group table and sat down for the service.

That was the morning I let myself notice it.

I want to be careful with what I am saying here.

I am not telling you that Rosabella climbed those stairs or carried that casserole.

I had been doing other things alongside the supplement.

I had been walking a little more deliberately after my conversation with Dr. Tanaka.

I had been taking the steps in our own house at home, on purpose, three times a day, rather than avoiding them.

Something had shifted in me the afternoon I drove home from her office in April that had nothing to do with a capsule on a saucer.

There were several things changing at once.

The way there are when a careful woman finally tells a careful doctor the thing she has been carrying alone for two years.

What I can say is that I added one small thing to a list of small things I was newly doing.

I kept everything else exactly the same — including the medication Dr. Tanaka has me on.

And the months that followed were qualitatively different from the eighteen months that preceded them.

I saw Dr. Tanaka in October.

New bloodwork. New EKG.

She read the results for a few minutes.

Then she looked up.

She said: "Your numbers are in the same good place they've been. But you look like someone who hasn't been measuring her own breath lately."

I told her about the stairs and the casserole.

I told her about the beetroot.

I told her I'd called the office before I started.

She nodded.

She said: "That was the right way to do it. Antioxidant support in this range is well-researched. Keep going."

She did not change my medication.

She did not change anything else.

She told me she'd see me in March.

I drove home from her office that afternoon thinking that there is a quiet, careful conversation happening between a woman's body and her doctor in the years after the change.

And that the bloodwork only tells half of it.

The other half is what the body tells you on the stairs.

I'm telling you this because I think you might be a woman where I was two springs ago.

Post-menopausal.

In fine shape on paper.

On a medication that's doing its job.

With a quiet, persistent awareness that something has shifted in the way your body handles the small physical work of your day — the stairs, the slopes, the things you used to do without noticing them.

If that's you, please don't ignore what your body is telling you just because your chart is fine.

The chart is one piece of information.

Your body is another.

A good doctor wants both.

Mine asked me directly, and I'm grateful she did.

If yours hasn't, you can be the one to bring it up.

If you decide to think about adding a structure-function supplement to what you're already doing, please make the call before you start.

If you're on any cardiovascular medication — cholesterol, blood pressure, blood thinner, anything — this is not optional.

Call your doctor's office.

Call your pharmacist.

The two minutes it takes is the most important two minutes of your engagement with anything you put in your body.

The brand I added to my morning is Rosabella.

Two capsules with water, alongside my prescription, with my coffee.

1,300 milligrams of organic beetroot per serving, cold-pressed at low temperatures.

It is not a fix for anything.

It is not a substitute for hormone therapy or any cardiovascular medication.

It is not a treatment for menopause or for post-menopausal anything.

It is one small thing some women add to the long list of things they are already doing for themselves, alongside everything else.

Bodies are different.

Some women notice something within the first two weeks.

Some women take a couple of months.

Some women don't notice much at all.

That is the truthful version, and I would rather you hear it from me now than be disappointed later.

P.S. Rosabella has a ninety-day satisfaction guarantee.

Any reason at all.

You don't have to prove anything to anyone — not to them, not about your numbers, not about how you feel.

If you tried it and you're not satisfied, you're not satisfied.

They refund you.

That is the whole policy.

I appreciate that kind of policy at sixty-four.

P.P.S. They're a small operation working with a farming community in Lancaster County.

The cold-pressing takes longer than the conventional methods, so they sometimes run out between harvests.

I keep an extra bottle in the cupboard now.

I learned to stay one bottle ahead.

P.P.P.S. If you take only one thing from this letter, please take this:

Call your doctor's office and your pharmacist before you add anything new to a cardiovascular medication.

Not because anything bad will happen — probably nothing will.

Because the people who know your chart are the right people to confirm the addition is sensible for you.

The call takes two minutes.

Your doctor's office wants the call.

Your pharmacist wants the call.

Mine both said as much.

I am a sixty-two-year-old retired letter carrier in Vermont, and I am going to tell you about a coffee mug. 

My name is Edward.

People have called me Ed since I was nine years old.

I am sixty-two.

I live with my wife in a small house outside Burlington, Vermont.

I was a letter carrier for thirty-seven years.

I walked a rural route in northern Vermont for thirty-one of those years, and a smaller in-town route for the last six.

I retired at sixty after a hip surgery that healed cleanly.

I am not on any prescription medications.

My doctor — Dr. Foster, a careful woman I have been seeing for eleven years — tells me at every annual physical that my bloodwork is in the ranges she wants it in.

I sleep seven hours a night.

I walk my dog a mile and a quarter every morning.

I eat the way a man my age should eat, which is to say less than I want to and more vegetables than I would choose.

I am, on paper, a sixty-two-year-old man in fine shape.

That is the part I want you to hold while you read this.

I want to tell you, plainly, what I started to notice in the second winter after I retired.

It was small.

It would not have registered on any chart.

I am not telling you a dramatic story.

I am telling you what one careful man started to notice in his own kitchen on a cold morning when his wife was upstairs and the coffee was ready and he was the only one to notice it.

It started with my coffee mug.

I have a heavy stoneware mug that has been my morning mug for almost twenty-five years.

I bought it at a craft fair when our daughter was eleven.

It is the only mug I drink coffee out of in the morning.

I had been wrapping both hands around it for almost a year before I realized I was doing it.

I had not been doing that for the first twenty-three years I had the mug.

I had been holding it with one hand, the way most people hold a coffee mug, and reading the paper with the other.

Somewhere in there I had become a two-hand-around-the-mug man.

I noticed one morning that I was doing it.

I sat there at my own kitchen table and I asked myself, honestly, when I had started.

I could not tell you.

That was the first thing.

The second thing was the driveway.

I bring the mail in the afternoons, mostly out of habit.

A man who carried mail for thirty-seven years does not let his own mail sit in a box at the end of his driveway for very long.

Our driveway is about a hundred and forty yards.

It is unpaved.

It has a small hill in the middle of it.

I had been doing that walk in three or four minutes for as long as I had owned the house, which is twenty-six years.

Somewhere in the year before my second retirement winter, I had started to pause at the top of the hill.

I would stand there for thirty seconds with my hands in my pockets.

I would tell myself I was just looking at the trees.

I had not stood at the top of the driveway and looked at the trees in twenty-five years.

I had started to do it just about every afternoon.

I started to notice that, too.

The third thing was at the workbench.

I have a small workbench in our garage.

It is where I keep my hand tools, my coping saw, my small chisels, and the wood I am working on.

I am not a carpenter. I do small things — picture frames, a small step stool for our granddaughter, a birdhouse for the kitchen window — that take an evening or two.

I had been doing that work at the workbench in the garage on Saturday mornings for as long as we had lived in the house.

In the winter, the garage is cold. I have a small electric heater I run when I am out there.

Two winters ago, I started having trouble working the tools.

My hands were cold in the garage even with the heater going.

I started having to come back into the kitchen to warm them up every twenty minutes.

I stopped going out there.

The workbench sat empty for fourteen months.

I told my wife it was that I had run out of small projects.

I had not run out of small projects.

I had a piece of pine in there I had wanted to make into a small box for almost a year.

I had not gone out and made it.

I sat with all of this for a winter.

I went in for my annual physical in March.

Dr. Foster did the bloodwork.

I sat across from her at her desk a week later and we went over the results.

Everything was in the ranges she wanted it in.

Not borderline. Not perfect. In the ranges.

She looked at me at the end.

She said: anything we should be talking about, Ed?

I told her about the coffee mug.

I told her about the driveway.

I told her about the workbench.

I told her my hands felt different than they had at fifty-five, and that I was not asking for an answer, but I wanted her to have the picture.

She listened.

She wrote it down.

She said: I do not see anything on your chart I would do anything about today. But I appreciate you telling me. That is your body and you know it better than I do. If something changes, or if you want to come back sooner, you call me.

I told her thank you.

I left.

I drove home thinking about that conversation.

What I drove home thinking was this.

The chart was not wrong.

I was not wrong either.

There is a kind of slow shift that does not show up on the chart yet.

A man in his early sixties can be in fine shape by every measure his doctor can take and still be noticing that his hands are different in his own kitchen.

It is not a thing to panic about.

It is not a thing to ignore.

It is, I decided that afternoon, a thing to pay attention to.

I started reading.

I read for almost three weeks.

I am, by temperament, a careful man.

A man who walks rural mail routes for thirty-one years is, almost by definition, a careful man.

I do not put anything into my body without understanding what I am putting in.

I read about beetroot.

I read about the deep crimson pigment in the root.

It is called betalain.

It is the color that stains your cutting board for three days.

It is also, according to a long and patient body of research the cardiovascular community has been quietly building for decades, one of the more potent antioxidants in the food supply.

I read about the long, slowly built relationship the structure-function research has had with this vegetable.

In the context of supporting healthy circulation.

Supporting cardiovascular wellness.

Supporting the body's own cellular energy.

In people whose numbers are already in the range their doctors want them in.

None of what I read was a fireworks story.

It was the opposite of one.

It was a vegetable that has been quietly studied for a long time by careful people who were not making outlandish claims.

The language being used in the careful, structure-function corner of the conversation was the language I had been waiting to find.

Supports healthy circulation.

Not improves circulation.

Not fixes cold hands.

Not treats anything.

Just supports.

In people whose numbers are already in the ranges their doctors want them in.

That was the shape of the language I had been hoping to find.

I came across a small company called Rosabella.

I want to tell you what I liked about it, plainly.

The first thing was the chemistry.

The betalain pigment is heat-sensitive.

If you process beetroot the standard way, with heat, you lose some of the compound to the heat.

Rosabella cold-presses at low temperatures because they want as much of the compound as possible to make it from the field into the capsule.

That is a slower way to make a supplement.

It costs them more.

They made the choice anyway.

At sixty-two, after thirty-seven years of watching small businesses on my route open and close and the slow careful ones outlast the loud ones, I have come to pay close attention to the small decisions companies make when no one is watching.

Those decisions tell you everything.

The second thing I liked was the simplicity.

Two capsules in the morning with a glass of water.

That is the whole protocol.

I am a sixty-two-year-old retired letter carrier.

I am not interested in a protocol that requires its own shelf.

Now I am going to tell you the most important part of this letter, the part I want every man my age who reads this to hear.

Before I added anything to what I was taking, I called my pharmacist.

His name is Stephen.

He has been the pharmacist at the small independent pharmacy in our town for twelve years.

I have been filling things there for about as long.

I called him for a specific reason.

I am on no prescription medications.

I take a multivitamin and a vitamin D and that is the entirety of my pill organizer.

I called Stephen anyway.

I called because I am a careful man and because I wanted somebody who knew what was on my record to confirm that adding a beetroot capsule to a multivitamin was the right thing for a man my age to do.

He pulled up my profile.

He confirmed there were no concerns at the dosage on the label.

He told me to take it in the morning with my coffee the way the bottle suggested.

He told me to call him back if Dr. Foster ever started me on any prescription medication in the future, because the conversation would be different at that point.

He told me, before he got off the phone, that he wished more of his patients made the call I had just made.

I also called Dr. Foster's office that week.

I left a message with her nurse, with the same information.

She called me back the next day to tell me Dr. Foster had no concerns about my adding it at the dosage on the label.

I want to say something now, plainly, to every reader of this letter.

If you are on any prescription medication, especially anything cardiovascular, please make the call before you add anything new.

I called Stephen.

I called Dr. Foster's office.

I would have made a third call if I had been on a prescription.

So should you.

The two minutes the call takes is the most important two minutes of your engagement with any supplement.

I started on a Monday in April.

I set the bottle on the counter next to the coffee maker.

I took the two capsules with my first sip of coffee.

The first two weeks I felt nothing in particular.

I had read enough to know that bodies are different.

Some men notice something within the first week or two.

Some men take much longer.

Some men don't notice much at all.

I was prepared to land in any of those camps.

I was prepared to be honest with myself about the result.

What I noticed, I noticed slowly.

The first thing I noticed, around week six, was the coffee mug.

It was a Wednesday morning.

I was at the kitchen table.

The paper was open in front of me.

I had my coffee in my right hand.

My left hand was on the paper.

I sat with that for a moment.

I had not been a one-hand-on-the-mug man for almost a year.

I had been quietly two-handing my coffee for that long.

I had not, that morning, gone to put my second hand on the mug.

I had been reading the front page for ten minutes with the paper in my left hand and the mug in my right.

I did not say anything about it.

I did not want to make a thing of it.

I had read enough not to make a thing of one observation.

I made a quiet note.

I kept paying attention.

The second thing I noticed, around week nine, was the driveway.

It was a Sunday afternoon in early May.

I went out to bring in the mail.

I walked down the driveway with my hands at my sides.

I went to the box, took the mail out, turned around, and walked back up.

I came in the kitchen door.

My wife was at the table.

She asked me what I was smiling about.

I had not realized I was smiling.

I had not paused at the top of the hill.

I had walked up the driveway at the speed I had walked up the driveway for twenty-five years.

I had not done that in over a year.

I told her about the coffee mug, too.

She told me she had been waiting for me to notice the coffee mug.

She had been watching me, the way wives watch.

The third thing I noticed was the workbench.

It was a Saturday morning in late May.

It was cool outside but not cold.

The garage door was closed.

I had the small electric heater going.

I had the piece of pine I had been wanting to make into a small box for our daughter sitting on the workbench.

I worked on it for an hour and a half.

Without gloves.

My hands worked the coping saw and the chisels the way they had worked them for thirty years.

When I came in for lunch, my wife looked at the sawdust on my sleeves and she did not say anything.

She does not say anything about most things she notices.

She just sees them.

There's a Flight of Stairs at My Granddaughter's School I'd Been Quietly Losing to for Two Years.

There's a flight of stairs at my granddaughter's elementary school.

Two flights, actually — twenty-two steps each, with a small landing between them.

The auditorium where her spring piano recital happens every May is at the top.

For her first three recitals I climbed those stairs without thinking about them.

At her fourth recital, two springs ago, I stopped on the landing.

Just for a moment.

I told myself I was admiring a watercolor that some fifth grader had taped to the wall.

At her fifth recital, last spring, I stopped on the landing again.

And then halfway up the second flight.

By the time I got to the auditorium I sat in my seat for two minutes before I could really focus on the program.

A man at least ten years older than me passed me on the way up.

He didn't stop.

I sat there in the auditorium that afternoon listening to children play "Für Elise" with one hand, and I made myself a small, careful promise.

The next time I saw my cardiologist, I was going to tell her about the stairs.

She looked across her desk at me a week later and said something I think about almost every day now.

She said: "Marilyn. Your bloodwork is in the ranges I want it in. Your medication is doing what we hoped it would do. But you're three years past the change now, and the conversation I want to have with you today is a little different from the ones we've been having."

Then she sat back in her chair and asked me — and these are her exact words — "what your body has been telling you that you haven't been telling me."

I sat there for a long moment.

I am sixty-four years old.

I was a high school guidance counselor for twenty-eight years before I retired.

I have spent most of my professional life asking other people careful questions and waiting for them to answer honestly.

I had not expected to be on the receiving end of one.

But I had walked into her office that morning prepared to answer one.

I told her about the stairs.

That was one thing.

The other thing was the hill to our church.

We've gone to the same small church for thirty-one years.

The parking lot is on the lower lawn. The church itself is at the top of a small slope — maybe forty paces, an incline you wouldn't call a hill if you were younger than fifty.

I had walked that slope every Sunday morning for thirty-one years without registering it.

Sometime in the year before my granddaughter's fifth recital, I had started parking in the upper lot instead, by the office.

It's only twenty paces, on the flat.

I told myself it was easier when I was carrying a casserole for the Sunday lunch.

I had not been carrying a casserole every Sunday for thirty-one years.

I had been carrying my body up a slope that was telling me something I was not in a mood to hear.

There was one more thing I told her too.

The way I sometimes notice my own heartbeat at night now, the way I didn't notice it ten years ago.

Not loudly. Not alarmingly.

Just present in a way it hadn't been present before.

I told her I knew none of it was anything, exactly.

My numbers were in the ranges. The medication was doing its work. The annual EKG had been unremarkable.

I told her I was probably making something out of nothing.

She did not say I was making something out of nothing.

She said: "Your numbers are in good shape, Marilyn. The medication is doing what we wanted it to do. A lot of women in their sixties start paying closer attention to what their bodies are telling them, especially the small physical things they used to do without noticing. That's a healthy instinct. The chart is one piece of information. What you've just told me is another. Both are useful."

She was quiet for a moment after that.

Then she said: "I'm not going to change anything today. Your medication is doing what we want it to do. But if you'd like to think about whether there's one small additive thing you might bring into your mornings — something general, structure-function, well-researched, that supports what your body is already doing — I'd be happy to have that conversation at your next appointment. Or sooner, if you call."

I drove home from her office that afternoon thinking about the difference between a doctor who treats a chart and a doctor who reads a person.

I started reading the next day.

I want to be clear about something before I describe what I read.

I am not a doctor.

I am not a nurse.

I do not have any medical expertise of any kind.

I am a retired high school guidance counselor with a husband, two grown sons, a granddaughter who plays piano, and a relationship with my own body that I have been paying closer attention to in recent years.

What follows is one careful woman's experience told the way I'd tell a friend at the kitchen table.

Nothing more.

I am a careful reader.

Twenty-eight years of working with teenagers and their parents teaches you to be suspicious of anything that arrives loud.

I skipped past the supplement companies promising things no supplement can promise.

I skipped past the protocols and the morning routines and the influencers and the people selling certainty.

I was looking for the careful, quiet, structure-function corner of the conversation.

The thing that kept coming up there was beetroot.

Not beetroot juice. Not the beetroot powders at the health food store.

The active compound in beetroot — a family of pigments called betalains.

The research is real.

It has been built carefully over decades by careful people who have not, to their credit, been making outlandish claims.

The betalains in beetroot are studied in the context of supporting healthy circulation, supporting cardiovascular wellness, and supporting the body's natural antioxidant balance — particularly in people whose numbers are already in the range their doctor wants them in.

That was the shape of the language I had been looking for.

Not "fixes." Not "treats." Not "reverses."

Just supports.

In women whose cardiovascular wellness was part of the long, careful conversation they were having with their own doctors.

One brand kept showing up in the more careful sources.

Rosabella.

The thing I liked about it was a small thing.

Betalains are heat-sensitive — that's just the chemistry of the molecule — and most processing methods involve high temperatures, which is the cheaper and faster way.

Rosabella cold-presses their beetroot at low temperatures.

It costs them more to do it that way.

It takes longer.

They do it anyway, because more of the active compound survives the processing.

After twenty-eight years of telling teenagers to pay attention to what people do rather than what they say, I have come to believe the same thing about companies.

The other thing I liked was the simplicity.

Two capsules in the morning with a glass of water.

That is the whole protocol.

I am sixty-four years old. I have already lived through the years of complicated routines.

I am not interested in another one.

Before I added it, I made two phone calls.

I want to talk about both, because they are the most important part of any honest letter about a supplement.

I am on a low-dose cholesterol medication. I have been on it for eight years.

My doctor and I are happy with how it has been working.

I would not add anything to that medication without checking first whether the addition was sensible — and "sensible" is not a thing I, as a retired guidance counselor in suburban Pittsburgh, am qualified to determine on my own.

I called Dr. Tanaka's office on a Tuesday morning.

I left a message with her nurse with the brand name and the dosage.

She called me back that afternoon.

Dr. Tanaka had said it was a reasonable thing to add, that there were no concerns with the dosage on the label alongside my current medication, and to let her know how I was feeling at our next appointment.

I called the pharmacy where I have been filling my prescription for nine years.

I told the pharmacist on duty what I wanted to add.

She pulled up my profile.

She confirmed what Dr. Tanaka had said — no concerns at the dosage on the label, given the medication and dose I am on.

She told me to take the capsules at the same time I take my morning prescription, with water, the way the bottle suggested.

Both calls together took me less than ten minutes.

If you are on any cardiovascular medication — a cholesterol medication, a blood pressure medication, a blood thinner, anything — those minutes are the most important minutes you will spend on the supplement.

I am not the person to ask.

New bloodwork. New EKG.

Post-menopausal.

In fine shape on paper.

On a medication that's doing its job.

With a quiet, persistent awareness that something has shifted in the way your body handles the small physical work of your day — the stairs, the slopes, the things you used to do without noticing them.
